Ash'ari's First Trait: Fairness and Objectivity
Editor identifies Ash'ari's first trait: fairness, citing his book introduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ash'ari's fairness and objectivity are demonstrated through the introduction to his seminal work Maqalat al-Islamiyyin.

Core Idea
Fairness in scholarship is proven by Ash'ari's own words.

Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is renowned for his balanced approach to theology. The first trait highlighted by his biographers is his fairness and objectivity. The strongest proof is the introduction to his book Maqalat al-Islamiyyin, where he sets out to present the views of different Islamic sects impartially. This passage introduces that evidence and shows how Ash'ari's methodology was grounded in intellectual honesty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice how the editor directly states the trait and then provides a specific evidence: the introduction to Maqalat al-Islamiyyin. The quote from the introduction begins but is incomplete here—imagine the scholarly care behind those words.
The quote starts with 'I saw people in...' which hints at Ash'ari's observational and impartial stance.
2
Understand the trait: Fairness and Objectivity
Consider why 'fairness' (الإنصاف) is listed as a personality trait. In theological debates, it means presenting opposing views accurately without bias. Objectivity (الموضوعية) complements this by focusing on facts rather than personal preference.
3
Link the evidence to the trait
Ask: How does the introduction to Maqalat al-Islamiyyin prove fairness? The full introduction is known to be Ash'ari's statement of method: he describes seeing people divided and decides to record their differences fairly. The fragment here is the beginning of that statement.
This connects to the learning objective—seeing how a written work can embody a personal quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما سمات شخصيته التي تميز بها، فهي :
١ الإنصاف والموضوعية :
وأقوى شاهد على هذا الملمح مقدمته الرائعة لكتابه «مقالات الإسلاميين واختلافات المصلين ؛ إذ يقول(۲) : ورأيت الناس في
As for the traits of his personality by which he was distinguished, they are: 1. Fairness and objectivity: The strongest evidence for this characteristic is his wonderful introduction to his book 'Maqalat al-Islamiyyin wa Ikhtilaf al-Musallin', as he says (2): 'I saw people in...'
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the first specific personality trait of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari as presented by the editor: fairness and objectivity (الإنصاف والموضوعية). The editor states that the strongest evidence for this trait is the introduction to Ash'ari's work 'Maqalat al-Islamiyyin wa Ikhtilaf al-Musallin' (The Doctrines of the Muslims and the Differences of the Worshippers). The passage then begins a direct quotation from that introduction, which starts with 'I saw people in...' (ورأيت الناس في). The quote is cut off due to the page boundary, but it is meant to illustrate his fair and objective approach to presenting different theological views.

Key Terms
الإنصاف
الموضوعية
مقالات الإسلاميين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Ash'ari's fairness was merely a rhetorical claim without concrete evidence.
Correction: The editor explicitly points to a specific textual evidence: the introduction to Maqalat al-Islamiyyin, which is a real work where Ash'ari demonstrates his impartial methodology.

Checkpoint
Which book's introduction is cited as the strongest evidence for Ash'ari's fairness?
A) Al-Luma'
B) Al-Ibanah
C) Maqalat al-Islamiyyin
D) Al-Insaf

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari's fairness and objectivity are clearly evidenced by the introduction to his work Maqalat al-Islamiyyin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you present the views of someone you disagree with, do you do so with the same commitment to fairness that Imam Ash'ari displayed? Examine your own habits in debates or discussions. Are you truly objective like the introduction of 'Maqalat al-Islamiyyin' demands, or do you subtly distort?
?
The editor calls Ash'ari's introduction 'wonderful' because of its fairness. How wonderful is your own speech when describing others' beliefs? Would someone reading your words find evidence of fairness, or of bias?
?
Consider a recent disagreement you had. Did you accurately represent the other person's position before responding? If not, what would it take for you to adopt Ash'ari's method of first understanding before judging?

The Life and Education of Imam al-Ash'ari
Biographical introduction of Imam al-Ash'ari

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early life, teachers, and scholarly formation of Imam al-Ash'ari, and how his education prepared him to become a leading theologian.

Core Idea
Imam al-Ash'ari's broad education from many teachers shaped his mastery of diverse Islamic sciences.

This passage opens with the full name and lineage of Imam al-Ash'ari, tracing him back to a Companion of the Prophet. It then describes his birth in Basra, his move to Baghdad, and his extensive studies under many shaykhs—both those he met in person and those he learned from indirectly. Understanding his early life helps us appreciate the foundation of his later contributions to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the lineage and birth details
Notice how the author traces his lineage back to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, a Companion. Note the year and place of birth.
2
Identify the types of teachers
The passage distinguishes between direct shaykhs (those he met) and indirect shaykhs (those he learned from without meeting). Reflect on how this broad network enriched his knowledge.
3
Consider his specialization
Though he studied many fields, he specialized in kalam. Think about how his comprehensive background might have influenced his theological method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللُّمَع في الردّ على أَهلِ الزَّيْغِ والبدع والتبسط مع التلاميذ والأصحاب، مقرونة بشواهد سريعة لكل سمة من هذه السمات، فيما يلي : ولد شيخنا علي بن إسماعيل بن أبي بشر إسحاق بن سالم بن إسماعيل بن عبد الله بن بلال بن أبي بردة بن أبي موسى الأشعري، صاحب رسول الله -صلى الله عليه وسلم- في البصرة عام (٢٦٠هـ)، وعاش بها أكثر سني حياته ، ثم انتقل إلى بغداد، وأقام بها حتى لقي ربه، في قرابة الخامسة والستين من العمر، ولقي الكثيرين من الشيوخ في عاصمتي الثقافة الإسلامية حينذاك، وقرأ للكثيرين، وسمع عنهم وإن لم يلقهم، وتأثر بهم ؛ فله شيوخ مباشرون، وشيوخ غير مباشرين . كما يلاحظ أنه عن طريق هؤلاء الشيوخ بنوعيهم، وعن طريق القراءة والدراسة والسماع أيضًا، تيسر له الإلمام بتيارات الفكر الإسلامي المتنوعة، وسائر الاتجاهات والمذاهب في مختلف حقول المعرفة الذائعة في عصره، وإن اختص بعلم الكلام والدراسات العقلية، كما تدل مؤلفاته (۱) ، وإنتاجه العلمي الغزير .
Al-Luma' fi al-Radd 'ala Ahl al-Zaygh wa al-Bida' (The Gleams in Refuting the People of Deviation and Heresy) and the extension with students and companions, accompanied by quick evidences for each of these traits, as follows: Our Shaykh 'Ali ibn Isma'il ibn Abi Bishr Ishaq ibn Salim ibn Isma'il ibn 'Abd Allah ibn Bilal ibn Abi Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari, the Companion of the Messenger of God – may God bless him and grant him peace – was born in Basra in the year (260 AH), and lived there most of his life, then moved to Baghdad, and resided there until he met his Lord, at about sixty-five years of age. He met many shaykhs in the two capitals of Islamic culture at that time, and read under many, and heard from them even if he did not meet them, and was influenced by them; thus he had direct shaykhs and indirect shaykhs. It is also noted that through these shaykhs of both types, and through reading, study, and hearing as well, he was enabled to become familiar with the diverse currents of Islamic thought, and all the trends and schools in various fields of knowledge prevalent in his era, although he specialized in kalam and rational studies, as his works and his abundant scholarly output indicate.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage introduces the life and education of Imam al-Ash'ari. It begins with the title of the book and then presents the author's full lineage, tracing his ancestry to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, a Companion of the Prophet. It states that he was born in Basra in 260 AH, lived most of his life there, then moved to Baghdad and died at about 65. The passage emphasizes his broad education: he met many shaykhs in Basra and Baghdad, studied under many, and was influenced by them, both directly and indirectly. Through this extensive network, he gained comprehensive knowledge of the diverse Islamic intellectual currents of his time, though he specialized in kalam and rational studies, as evidenced by his many works.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ash'ari moved from Basra to Baghdad to seek knowledge. What physical or comfort sacrifices have you made—or are you willing to make—to deepen your understanding of the deen? Are you too attached to your current location or routine to prioritize learning?
?
He studied under many teachers, both direct and indirect. This means he actively sought out diverse sources, not just one. In your own Islamic learning, do you rely on a single teacher or source, or do you actively seek out multiple perspectives? What might you be missing by staying in one comfort zone?
?
Despite mastering many fields, al-Ash'ari specialized in kalam. What is the one field of Islamic knowledge that you feel called to specialize in? Are you giving it the time and focus it deserves, or are you distracted by less important matters? What step will you take this week to move closer to that specialization?

Life of Imam al-Ashari

Al-Ash'ari's Objectivity
Al-Ash'ari enumerates faults of previous writers and states his own aim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari's introduction to Maqalat al-Islamiyyin demonstrates his commitment to objectivity and fair scholarship.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's objectivity set a new standard for scholarly fairness.

This lesson focuses on the first trait: al-Ash'ari's remarkable objectivity. He criticizes previous writers for bias and inaccuracies, and his own introduction shows his dedication to accurate reporting. The passage from his book is a powerful testimony.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the list of faults al-Ash'ari identifies.
2
Identify the five faults
List the five types of writers al-Ash'ari criticizes. Notice the progression from carelessness to deliberate misrepresentation.
The faults are: 1) falls short, 2) errs, 3) deliberately lies, 4) lacks thoroughness, 5) adds false statements.
3
Reflect on his stance
Consider why al-Ash'ari says this is not the way of the learned. What does this reveal about his own methodology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما سمات شخصيته التي تميز بها ، فهي :
١ الإنصاف والموضوعية :
وأقوى شاهد على هذا الملمح مقدمته الرائعة لكتابه «مقالات الإسلاميين واختلافات المصلين ؛ إذ يقول(۱) : ورأيت الناس في حكاية ما يحكون من ذِكْرِ المقالات، ويصنفون في النحل والديانات : من بين مقصر فيما يحكيه ، وغالط فيما يذكره من قول مخالفيه، ومن بين متعمد للكذب في الحكاية؛ إرادةَ التَّشنيع على من يخالفه، ومن بين تارك للتقصي في روايته لما يرويه من اختلاف المختلفين، ومن بين من يضيف إلى قول مخالفيه ما يظن أن الحجة تلزمهم به. وليس هذا سبيل الربانيين، ولا سبيل الفطناء المميزين ؛ فحداني ما رأيت من ذلك على شرح ما التمست شرحه من أمر المقالات».
-٢- الزهد وبساطة العيش :
ذكروا أنه كان يعيش على رَيْعِ وَقْفٍ أهلي؛ لأحد أجداده، وهو بلال ابن أبي بردةَ آخرِ الآباء الستة بين إمامنا وبين أبي موسى الأشعري -رضي الله عنه - عنه - وكانت نفقته في السنة سبعة عشر درهما
أحمد
وروى ابن عساکر بسنده عنه قال : سمعت أبا عمران موسى بن بن عليّ الفقيه ، قال : سمعت أبي يقول : خدمت الإمام أبا الحسن بالبصرة سنين، وعاشرته ببغداد إلى أن توفي رحمه الله- فلم أجد أورع منه ، ولا أغض طرفًا ، ولم أر شيخًا أكثر حياءً منه في أمور الدنيا ، ولا أنشط منه في أمور الآخرة» (۱) . وقال الذهبي – وهو ليس من أتباع الشيخ أو أنصاره ، فلشهادته قيمتها : « وكان قانعا متعففًا» (۲)
As for the traits of his personality that distinguished him, they are:
1. Objectivity and impartiality: The strongest evidence for this trait is his splendid introduction to his book 'Maqalat al-Islamiyyin wa Ikhtilaf al-Musallin', where he says: 'I saw that people, in narrating what they relate of the accounts of doctrines and classifying sects and religions, include: one who falls short in what he narrates, one who errs in what he mentions of the statement of his opponents, one who deliberately lies in the narration to disparage his opponents, one who neglects thoroughness in his transmission of the differences, and one who adds to his opponents' statements what he thinks makes the argument binding upon them. But this is not the way of the learned divines, nor the way of the intelligent discerning. So what I saw of that led me to explain what I was asked to explain concerning the subject of doctrines.'
2. Asceticism and simplicity of living: They mentioned that he lived on the revenue of a family endowment from one of his forefathers, namely Bilal ibn Abi Burda, the last of the six ancestors between our Imam and Abu Musa al-Ash'ari – may God be pleased with them – and his annual expenses were seventeen dirhams. Ibn 'Asakir narrated with his chain from him, saying: I heard Abu 'Imran Musa ibn 'Ali al-Faqih say: I heard my father say: I served Imam Abu al-Hasan in Basra for years, and I associated with him in Baghdad until he died – may God have mercy on him – and I never saw anyone more pious, nor with a more lowered gaze, and I never saw a shaykh more shy concerning worldly matters, nor more active in matters of the Hereafter.' Al-Dhahabi – and he is not a follower or supporter of the shaykh, so his testimony is valuable – said: 'He was content and abstemious.'
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage enumerates two of al-Ash'ari's key personal characteristics: objectivity and asceticism.
1) Objectivity and impartiality: The editor supports this by quoting al-Ash'ari's own introduction to his work 'Maqalat al-Islamiyyin'. In that passage, al-Ash'ari criticizes previous writers on sects for various failings: some are deficient in reporting, some make mistakes about opponents' views, some deliberately lie to discredit opponents, some neglect thoroughness, and some add statements to opponents to make their arguments easier to refute. He emphasizes that this is not the way of true scholars, and that his own aim is to provide an accurate account.
2) Asceticism and simplicity of living: The editor notes that al-Ash'ari lived on a family endowment and spent only seventeen dirhams per year. He then cites two testimonies: one from Ibn 'Asakir's chain, reporting that a servant found him the most pious and modest, and more active in religious matters; and another from al-Dhahabi, who despite not being a follower of al-Ash'ari, described him as content and abstemious, lending extra weight to the testimony.

Key Terms
الإنصاف
الموضوعية
مقالات الإسلاميين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari was a polemicist who distorted opponents' views.
Correction: His introduction explicitly condemns distortion and shows his commitment to fairness.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, which of the following is NOT a fault he observed in previous writers on sects?
A) Falling short in narration
B) Erring about opponents' statements
C) Correcting errors in transmission
D) Adding false claims to opponents

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's introduction reveals his insistence on accuracy and his rejection of common scholarly biases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you discuss a person or group you disagree with, which of the five faults listed by al-Ash'ari do you most often commit? Be honest. What one concrete step will you take today to repent from that fault?
?
Al-Ash'ari observed that many scholars deliberately lied to disparage opponents. Have you ever exaggerated or distorted someone's position to win an argument? What does your willingness to do that say about your heart? What will you do differently starting now?
?
Al-Ash'ari says that falling short in narration is not the way of learned divines. When you last conveyed a rumor or a partial truth, did you feel the weight of that responsibility? How will you ensure that from this day forward, you are among the 'intelligent discerning'?

Al-Ash'ari's Asceticism
Describes al-Ash'ari's ascetic lifestyle with financial and testimonial evidence.

By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extent of Imam al-Ash'ari's asceticism as described by his contemporaries.

Al-Ash'ari lived on seventeen dirhams a year, embodying true detachment.

This lesson covers al-Ash'ari's asceticism. He lived modestly on a family endowment, and testimonies from Ibn 'Asakir and al-Dhahabi confirm his piety and contentment. The passage presents financial details and eyewitness accounts.

Life of Imam al-Ashari
beginner
5 min
Imam Ashari’s Simple Living and Asceticism
Details of Ashari's frugal life from endowment income and contemporary witnesses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ashari’s frugal lifestyle and the testimonies of contemporaries confirm his piety and contentment.

Core Idea
Imam Ashari lived on just 17 dirhams a year, embodying true zuhd.

This passage reveals the personal side of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. While known for his theological legacy, his daily life was marked by extreme simplicity. Through the yield of a family endowment and the witness of those who knew him, we see a man devoted to the akhirah, not the dunya.

Guided Path
1
Understand the source of income
Ashari lived on the yield of a family endowment from his ancestor Bilal ibn Abi Burda. This shows he did not seek wealth from worldly sources.
The endowment was a waqf, a charitable trust.
2
Note the annual expenditure
His yearly expenses were only 17 dirhams, an extremely low amount indicating extreme asceticism.
For perspective, a dirham was a silver coin; 17 dirhams was barely enough for basic necessities.
3
Examine the testimony of Ibn Asakir's chain
Through a reliable chain, Abu 'Imran Musa ibn Ali's father, who served Ashari for years, testified to his unparalleled piety, modesty, shyness, and activity in worship.
The testimony covers both worldly shyness and eagerness for the hereafter.
4
Consider al-Dhahabi's independent testimony
Al-Dhahabi, not a follower of Ashari, confirms he was content (qani') and abstinent (muta'affif), adding objective credibility.
Al-Dhahabi is a later historian known for his critical assessments.
5
Reflect on the overall picture
The combination of his lifestyle and multiple witnesses paints a clear picture of a scholar who embodied Islamic virtues in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكروا أنه كان يعيش على رَيْعِ وَقْفٍ أهلي؛ لأحد أجداده، وهو بلال ابن أبي بردةَ آخرِ الآباء الستة بين إمامنا وبين أبي موسى الأشعري -رضي الله عنه - عنه - وكانت نفقته في السنة سبعة عشر درهما. وروى ابن عساکر بسنده عنه قال : سمعت أبا عمران موسى بن بن عليّ الفقيه ، قال : سمعت أبي يقول : خدمت الإمام أبا الحسن بالبصرة سنين، وعاشرته ببغداد إلى أن توفي رحمه الله- فلم أجد أورع منه ، ولا أغض طرفًا ، ولم أر شيخًا أكثر حياءً منه في أمور الدنيا ، ولا أنشط منه في أمور الآخرة. وقال الذهبي – وهو ليس من أتباع الشيخ أو أنصاره ، فلشهادته قيمتها : « وكان قانعا متعففًا».
They mentioned that he lived on the yield of a family endowment belonging to one of his grandfathers, namely Bilal ibn Abi Burda, the last of the six ancestors between our Imam and Abu Musa al-Ash'ari (may Allah be pleased with him) - and his annual expenditure was seventeen dirhams. And Ibn 'Asakir narrated with his chain from him, he said: I heard Abu 'Imran Musa ibn 'Ali al-Faqih, he said: I heard my father say: I served Imam Abu al-Hasan in Basra for years, and I associated with him in Baghdad until he died (may Allah have mercy on him) - and I did not find anyone more pious than him, nor more downcast of gaze, and I did not see a sheikh more shy in worldly matters than him, nor more active in matters of the Hereafter. And al-Dhahabi said - and he is not among the followers of the sheikh or his supporters, so his testimony has value: 'He was content and abstinent.'
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage provides biographical information about Imam Ashari's ascetic lifestyle. It states that he lived on the income of a family waqf (endowment) from his ancestor Bilal ibn Abi Burda. His annual expenses were only seventeen dirhams, indicating extreme frugality. Then two testimonies: Ibn Asakir transmits from Musa ibn Ali that his father served Ashari and testified to his piety, modesty, shyness, and activity in worship. Then al-Dhahabi, who is not a partisan of Ashari, confirms that he was content (qani') and abstinent (muta'affif). The author notes al-Dhahabi's independence to strengthen the credibility of the testimony.

ورع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ashari was a worldly scholar who sought fame and wealth.
Correction: Despite his fame, Ashari lived extremely simply, spending only 17 dirhams annually and being described as content and abstinent by unbiased witnesses.

Key Takeaway
Imam Ashari's austere lifestyle and the unanimous testimony of contemporaries confirm that he practiced what he preached.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
He was 'more active in matters of the Hereafter.' When you look at your last week, were you more active in pursuing deen or dunya? What one change can you make today to tip the balance toward the Hereafter?
?
Al-Dhahabi, who was not his follower, testified to his contentment. If someone who disagrees with you was asked about your character, would they testify to your contentment? What would they say? What would need to change for them to say that?

Ashari's Deathbed Refusal of Takfir
Ashari's deathbed testimony on not excommunicating Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Ashari's principle of avoiding takfir (excommunication) among Ahl al-Qibla, grounded in the unity of worship and the view that theological differences are only differences in expression.

Core Idea
Imam Ashari's deathbed testimony: No Muslim should be excommunicated because all worship the same God and differ only in words.

Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, on his deathbed, delivered a powerful testament to his moderate approach. He asked a witness to testify that he never declares any Muslim a disbeliever, regardless of their theological school. In this lesson, we examine his exact words and the reasoning behind them, which emphasize unity over division.

Guided Path
1
Read the deathbed testimony
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the context: Ashari's final words.
The narrator is Zahir al-Sarkhasi.
2
Understand key terms
Takfir means declaring someone a non-Muslim. Ahl al-Qibla refers to Muslims who pray towards Mecca. Grasp their significance.
3
Analyze Ashari's reasoning
Why does he say differences are merely in expressions? Reflect on the unity of worship being primary.
He argues that all Muslims point to the same worshipped being.
4
Consider implications
How does this statement guide inter-sectarian relationships? It promotes tolerance and avoids extremism.
5
Connect to Ashari's legacy
This deathbed testimony summarizes his centrist theology and commitment to avoiding takfir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتدال والوسطية :
اللُّمَع في الرد على أَهْلِ الزَّيْغِ والبدع
روى الشيخ زاهرُ السَّرخسي قال مات أبو الحسن الأشعري رحمه الله ورأسه في حجري ... ثم قال: «دعاني فأتيته فقال : «اشهد عليَّ أنَّي لا أُكَفِّر أحدًا من أهل هذه القبلة، لأن الكل يشيرون إلى معبود واحد وإنما هذا كله اختلاف العبارات».
Moderation and Centrism:
Al-Luma' fi al-Radd 'ala Ahl al-Zaygh wa al-Bida'
Al-Sheikh Zahir al-Sarkhasi narrated: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari died, may Allah have mercy on him, and his head was in my lap... then he said: 'He called me, so I came to him, and he said: "Bear witness that I do not excommunicate anyone from the people of this qibla, because all point to one worshipped being, and this is all just difference in expressions."'
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage records a famous deathbed statement of Imam Ashari. The narrator, Zahir al-Sarkhasi, was with him at death. Ashari asked him to bear witness that he does not takfir (excommunicate) anyone from Ahl al-Qibla (i.e., Muslims who pray towards Mecca). His reasoning is that all Muslims point to the same object of worship (ma'bud wahid); their differences are merely in expressions (ikhtilaf al-'ibarat). This shows his centrist position, avoiding extremism in takfir.

Key Terms
تكفير
أهل القبلة
اختلاف العبارات
اعتدال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ashari considered all theological differences as completely irrelevant or that he rejected any distinction between sects.
Correction: Ashari did not reject all distinctions; rather he refused to declare outright disbelief (kufr) based on differences in expression, maintaining that all Ahl al-Qibla are Muslims.

Checkpoint
What did Imam Ashari ask witnesses to testify about on his deathbed?
A) That he was a Sufi
B) That he did not takfir anyone from Ahl al-Qibla
C) That his book Al-Luma is authoritative
D) That he lived in poverty

Key Takeaway
Imam Ashari's principle: Do not excommunicate Muslims who worship the same God, even if they express their theology differently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever silently excommunicated another Muslim in your heart due to a difference in practice or belief? How does Ashari's deathbed testimony challenge you to reconsider that judgment?
?
Imam Ashari's final words prioritized unity over theological disagreement. What is one belief difference you hold with another Muslim that you now see as merely 'difference in expressions'? How can you act on this insight to heal a real relationship?

Imam Ashari's Cheerfulness with Students
Anecdote about Ashari's cheerful nature with students.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam Ashari, despite his theological depth, was known for his cheerful and humorous interactions with his students.

Core Idea
Imam Ashari combined deep theology with a joyful personality.

This brief but important anecdote from the biography of Imam Ashari reveals a dimension often overlooked: his approachability and humor. As we explore the passage, we see that the great theologian was not a stern figure but someone who joked freely with his companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation

2
Identify the source of the report
The text names Muhammad ibn Ishaq al-Nadim as the narrator. Consider: why would a biographer include such a detail? What does it say about Ashari's character?
Al-Nadim was a known historian and bibliographer.
3
Reflect on the broader lesson
Think about how this anecdote counters the stereotype of theologians as always stern. How might Ashari's humor have helped his students engage with difficult topics?
This connects to the misconception that scholars of kalam lacked warmth.

?

?

Ashari's Spirituality and Sufi Connection
Describes Ashari as a master in both kalam and tasawwuf, with ascetic lifestyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam Ashari was not only a master theologian but also a deeply spiritual ascetic and Sufi, and how this dimension complements his kalam legacy.

Core Idea
Ashari's spirituality was as profound as his theology.

This passage reveals a lesser-known side of Imam Ashari: his deep involvement in Sufism and asceticism. It corrects the misconception that he was purely a rational theologian, showing his holistic religious practice. We'll explore how his personal life reflected his spiritual state.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the claims made about Ashari's spiritual status and lifestyle.
2
Identify key details
Notice the specifics: his celibacy, his contentment with little, and his students' emulation of his asceticism.
These details paint a picture of a man who lived his faith.

This challenges the common view that kalam and tasawwuf are opposed.

قالوا : وكان الشيخ سيدا - رضي الله عنه- في التصوف واعتبارِ القلوب، كما هو سيد في علم الكلام وأصناف العلوم، وقد يتصل بتصوفه وحاله الروحية إيثاره العزوبة، فلم يعرف له ولد ولا زوجة، كحال كثيرين من خواص العلماء، وتقلله من الدنيا . واكتفاؤه بدراهم معدودات من ريع وقف لأجداده. ومما يؤكد ذلك أن أقرب تلاميذه إليه ،عُرِفوا ،مثله بالزهد والتقلل من الدنيا ، والنشاط في الذكر والعبادة.
His Spirituality and Sufism:
They said: The sheikh was a master (may Allah be pleased with him) in Sufism and the consideration of hearts, just as he was a master in kalam and various sciences. And connected to his Sufism and spiritual state was his preference for celibacy, for he was not known to have had a child or a wife, like the condition of many of the elite scholars, and his having little of the world. And his contenting himself with a few dirhams from the yield of his ancestors' endowment. Commentary
This passage emphasizes Imam Ashari's spiritual dimension. It states that he was a master (sayyid) in tasawwuf and 'i'tibar al-qulub (consideration of hearts) as well as in kalam. His spiritual state included i'thar al-'uzuba (preference for celibacy) – he had no known wife or children. He was content with minimal worldly goods from the endowment. His students emulated his asceticism and devotion.

Imam Ashari's Refusal to Excommunicate Fellow Muslims
Ashari's deathbed testimony against excommunication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam Abul Hasan al-Ash'ari explicitly refused to excommunicate any Muslim on his deathbed, affirming that all people of the qibla worship the same God.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's final words: no Muslim is excommunicated despite differing expressions.

This lesson presents a poignant narration from Imam al-Ash'ari's final moments. On his deathbed, he made a clear statement about his stance on takfir (excommunication). His words provide a crucial insight into the spirit of classical Sunni theology, emphasizing unity over division.

Guided Path
1
Read the deathbed narration
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Let the words of Imam Ashari sink in before analyzing them.
The narration is a firsthand account by a companion.
2
Identify the two key claims
Ashari makes two points: first, he does not excommunicate anyone from the people of the qibla; second, the reason is that all point to one God, and differences are only in expressions.
3
Reflect on the significance of this stance
Consider why Ashari chose to make this statement on his deathbed. How does it shape our understanding of his theological project?
This shows his commitment to unity despite theological disagreements.
4
Address the common misconception
Some have claimed that Ashari excommunicated certain Muslims. The guided breakdown will show how this passage refutes that.
The misconception is directly countered by Ashari's own words.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
روى الشيخ زاهرُ السَّرخسي قال مات أبو الحسن الأشعري الله ورأسه في حجري ... ثم قال: «دعاني فأتيته فقال : «اشهد عليَّ أنَّي لا أُكَفِّر أحدًا من أهل هذه القبلة، لأن الكل يشيرون إلى معبود واحد وإنما هذا كله اختلاف العبارات .

Then he said: "He called me, so I came to him, and he said: Bear witness upon me that I do not excommunicate anyone from the people of this qibla, because all point to one worshipped deity, and this is all only a difference of expressions."'

Commentary
This is a biographical narration about Imam Ashari's deathbed statement. It reports that he asked his witness to affirm that he does not excommunicate (takfir) any Muslim, because all people of the qibla worship the same God, and theological differences are only variations in expression. This passage highlights Ashari's position against sectarian excommunication and his emphasis on the unity of the Muslim community despite doctrinal differences.

Ashari's Cheerfulness with Students
Describes Ashari's cheerfulness and informality with students.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Imam Ashari combined deep scholarship with a light-hearted, humble approach.

The great theologian Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is often remembered for his intellectual contributions, but his personal character was equally remarkable. Historical accounts, like that of Muhammad ibn Ishaq al-Nadim, highlight his warmth and humor with his students. This passage introduces us to that side of Ashari, reminding us that true scholars are not stern but approachable.

Guided Path

Ash'ari's Work Ethic and Scholarly Influences
Details Ashari's prolific work habits and the role of his teachers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the factors behind Imam al-Ash'ari's remarkable scholarly productivity, including his work ethic, lifestyle choices, and the influence of his teachers.

Core Idea
Ash'ari's productivity came from disciplined devotion, not just genius.

This passage gives us a rare window into the personal habits and scholarly environment of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. It explains how he managed to produce an immense body of work despite a relatively short life, and highlights the role of his teachers in shaping his thought. As you read, notice the emphasis on perseverance and the avoidance of worldly distractions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

Pay attention to the detail that he had no wife, child, or official position.

٦ - دأبه ومثابرته على العمل : تأليفا، ومناظرة ،وتدريسًا ،وإملاء، وتراسلا مع أهم الأمصار الإسلامية في ،وقته على نحو يعجب الإنسان كيف تيسر له كل ذلك، في حياة لم تطل نسبيًّا ، ولكنها المثابرة والصبر على العمل، والاحتساب الذي يولد الحماسة المتواصلة، بالإضافة إلى توفره لعمله هذا، دون الانشغال بزوجة أو ولد أو وظيفة رسمية، وبعده عن الشئون السياسية إلا الكتابة النظرية عن الإمامة، ومؤلفاته الأصولية التي قد تلم بهذه الشئون ولكن على نحو تنظيري أيضًا، لا ينخرط في نشاط عملي قد يشغل صاحبه، بطبيعة الحال عن ، الإنتاج العلمي ويروي تلميذه ابن خفيف الشيرازي، المعروف بالشيخ الكبير قصة لقائه الأول بشيخه أبي الحسن بالبصرة، بأسلوب عاطفي جميل ، يبرز نشاط الشيخ المتواصل في شرح مذهبه، وفي المجادلة الدائبة مع خصومه إنها شخصية مثيرة، لا يطغى فيها جانب على آخر، ولعل ما تيسر للشيخ من صلات وعلاقات، وخاصة مع شيوخه الذين أخذ عنهم، وتأثر بهم ، هو الذي أنضج ملكاته، وصقل مواهبه، وصاغ أسلوب نشاطه وفكره، وهم رجال كُثر، سنكتفي هنا بالتعرض لأقربهم إليه .
6. His perseverance and persistence in work: authorship, debate, teaching, dictation, and correspondence with the most important Islamic cities in his time, in a manner that amazes one how he managed all that, in a life that was not long relatively, but it was perseverance and patience in work, and reckoning which generates continuous enthusiasm, in addition to his devotion to this work, without being occupied with a wife, child, or official position, and his distance from political affairs except for theoretical writing on the imamate, and his usul works which may touch on these affairs but also in a theoretical manner, not engaging in practical activity that might naturally occupy one from scientific production. And his student Ibn Khafif al-Shirazi, known as 'the Great Sheikh,' narrates the story of his first meeting with his teacher Abu al-Hasan in Basra, in a beautiful emotional style, which highlights the sheikh's continuous activity in explaining his doctrine and in constant argumentation with his opponents. It is an exciting personality, in which no aspect overwhelms another, and perhaps what was available to the sheikh of connections and relationships, especially with his teachers who he studied with and was influenced by, is what matured his faculties, polished his talents, and shaped his style of activity and thought. They are many men; we will suffice here by addressing the closest to him.

Commentary
This passage describes Ashari's extraordinary dedication to scholarship. He engaged in multiple scholarly activities simultaneously: writing, debating, teaching, dictating, and corresponding with scholars across the Islamic world. Despite a relatively short life, his perseverance and avoidance of distractions (no family, no political office) allowed him to achieve great productivity. The passage also notes that his student Ibn Khafif narrated an emotional encounter highlighting Ashari's constant activity in explaining and defending his doctrine. Finally, it reflects on Ashari's personality as balanced, and attributes his intellectual development to the many teachers he studied with and was influenced by. This section underscores the importance of mentorship and disciplined work in Ashari's scholarly formation.

٦ - دأبه ومثابرته على العمل : تأليفا، ومناظرة، وتدريسًا، وإملاء، وتراسلا مع أهم الأمصار الإسلامية في وقته على نحو يعجب الإنسان كيف تيسر له كل ذلك، في حياة لم تطل نسبيًّا، ولكنها المثابرة والصبر على العمل، والاحتساب الذي يولد الحماسة المتواصلة، بالإضافة إلى توفره لعمله هذا، دون الانشغال بزوجة أو ولد أو وظيفة رسمية، وبعده عن الشئون السياسية إلا الكتابة النظرية عن الإمامة، ومؤلفاته الأصولية التي قد تلم بهذه الشئون ولكن على نحو تنظيري أيضًا، لا ينخرط في نشاط عملي قد يشغل صاحبه، بطبيعة الحال عن الإنتاج العلمي. ويروي تلميذه ابن خفيف الشيرازي، المعروف بـ"الشيخ الكبير" قصة لقائه الأول بشيخه أبي الحسن بالبصرة، بأسلوب عاطفي جميل (١)، يبرز نشاط الشيخ المتواصل في شرح مذهبه، وفي المجادلة الدائبة مع خصومه.

إنها شخصية مثيرة، لا يطغى فيها جانب على آخر، ولعل ما تيسر للشيخ من صلات وعلاقات، وخاصة مع شيوخه الذين أخذ عنهم، وتأثر بهم، هو الذي أنضج ملكاته، وصقل مواهبه، وصاغ أسلوب نشاطه وفكره، وهم رجال كُثر، سنكتفي هنا بالتعرض لأقربهم إليه.
6. His habit and perseverance in work: authorship, debate, teaching, dictation, and correspondence with the most important Islamic regions of his time in a way that astonishes one how all that was possible for him, in a life that was relatively short, but it was perseverance and patience in work, and seeking reward (iḥtisāb) that generates continuous enthusiasm, in addition to his devotion to this work, without being occupied with a wife, child, or official position, and his distance from political affairs except for theoretical writing about the imamate, and his foundational works that may touch upon these affairs but also in a theoretical manner, not engaging in practical activity that would naturally occupy its owner from scientific production. And his student Ibn Khafīf al-Shīrāzī, known as 'al-Shaykh al-Kabīr', narrates the story of his first meeting with his teacher Abū al-Ḥasan in Basra in a beautiful emotional style (1), highlighting the shaykh's continuous activity in explaining his madhhab and in constant debate with his opponents.

It is an exciting personality, no one side dominates another, and perhaps what was available to the shaykh of connections and relations, especially with his teachers from whom he took and was influenced by, is what matured his faculties, polished his talents, and shaped the style of his activity and thought. They are many men, we will suffice here by presenting the closest to him.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a biographical description by the editor of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's work habits and personality. It begins by enumerating the various forms of his scholarly activity: authorship, debate, teaching, dictation, and correspondence with major Islamic centers. The editor marvels at how Ash'ari accomplished so much in a relatively short life, attributing it to perseverance, patience, and seeking divine reward (iḥtisāb), which generated continuous enthusiasm. Additionally, Ash'ari was not distracted by family (no wife or children) or an official government job, and he stayed away from practical politics, except for theoretical writing on the imamate. The editor then recounts a story from Ash'ari's student Ibn Khafīf about their first meeting in Basra, which highlights Ash'ari's tireless effort in explaining his doctrine and debating opponents. Finally, the editor reflects on Ash'ari's balanced personality, noting that his relationships with his teachers matured his abilities, and promises to introduce the most influential among them.

The Two Teachers Who Shaped Imam al-Ash'ari
The editor lists Ash'ari's two main teachers: al-Saji and al-Jubba'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari's early education under two very different teachers—a Sunni hadith scholar and a Mu'tazili theologian—prepared him for his later intellectual journey.

Core Idea
Imam al-Ash'ari was shaped by both Salafi hadith and Mu'tazili rationalism.

Imam al-Ash'ari did not emerge from a vacuum. His intellectual development was molded by two key figures: the Sunni hadith scholar al-Saji, who gave him his early Sunni foundation, and the leading Mu'tazili theologian al-Jubba'i, under whom he mastered rationalist theology. This biographical passage introduces these two teachers and sets the stage for Ash'ari's later transformation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the complete Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the two names and their descriptions.
The passage is from a scholarly introduction, so it uses honorifics like 'our shaykh'.
2
Contrast the two teachers
Note the differences: al-Saji is a "muḥaddith" (hadith scholar) and represents Sunni traditionalism; al-Jubba'i is a "mu'tazili" and represents rationalist theology.
Also notice Ash'ari's upbringing: first Sunni, then Mu'tazili after his mother's remarriage.
3
Reflect on Ash'ari's formation
Consider how Ash'ari's exposure to both schools of thought equipped him to later critique Mu'tazilism from a Sunni perspective.
This biographical background is key to understanding his later turn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - شيوخ أبي الحسن :

١ - الشيخ زكريا بن يحيى زكريا بن يحيى الساجي، محدث البصرة : كان شيخنا سُنّي النشأة: فهو قد وُلِدَ في بيت سني، لأب صالح متصل بعلماء الحديث الشريف، ولذا أوصى بولده، عندما أحس بقرب أجله - شيخ محدثي البصرة الشيخ زكريا بن يحيى الساجي (٣٠٧هـ) ، فكان تحت رعايته، وقد سمع منه أبو الحسن، وروى عنه بالسند المتصل - بعض الأحاديث، واستمدَّ عناصر ثقافته الأولى، وقد وصفه الذهبي - في سير أعلام النبلاء فقال: «كان من أئمة الحديث، أخذ منه أبو الحسن الأشعري مقالة السلف، في الصفات واعتمد عليها أبو الحسن في عدة تأليف (١) .

٢- الجبائي الأب، محمد بن عبد الوهاب البصري المعتزلي : نشأ شيخنا - إلى العاشرة تقريبًا - في بيئة سنية محافظة، وتشرب ثقافتها، ولكن زواج والدته بالشيخ محمد بن عبد الوهاب الجبائي (٣٠٣هـ)، شيخ المعتزلة بالبصرة ولسانهم الأول، نقله إلى بيئة أخرى يغلب عليها الاتجاه العقلي الاعتزالي، فاعتنق مذهب القوم وبرز في الدفاع عنه وناب عن شيخه الجبائي في المناظرات
C. The teachers of Abu al-Hasan:

1. al-Shaykh Zakariyya ibn Yahya Zakariyya ibn Yahya al-Saji, the muḥaddith of Basra: Our shaykh (Ash'ari) was of Sunni upbringing: he was born into a Sunni household, to a pious father connected to scholars of the noble hadith, and so he entrusted his son, when he felt his death approaching, to the shaykh of the muḥaddithūn of Basra, al-Shaykh Zakariyya ibn Yahya al-Saji (d. 307 AH). He was under his care, and Abu al-Hasan heard from him and narrated from him with a continuous chain (sanad muttaṣil) some hadiths, and derived the elements of his early culture. Al-Dhahabi described him in Siyar Aʿlām al-Nubalāʾ, saying: 'He was one of the imams of hadith. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari took from him the doctrine of the Salaf regarding the attributes, and relied upon it in several works (1).'

2. al-Jubba'i, the father, Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab al-Basri al-Mu'tazili: Our shaykh grew up - until around the age of ten - in a conservative Sunni environment and imbibed its culture. But the marriage of his mother to al-Shaykh Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab al-Jubba'i (d. 303 AH), the shaykh of the Mu'tazila in Basra and their foremost spokesman, transferred him to another environment dominated by the rationalist Mu'tazili orientation. So he adopted the doctrine of the people (the Mu'tazila) and excelled in defending it, and deputized for his teacher al-Jubba'i in debates.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage lists two primary teachers of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The first is Zakariyya ibn Yahya al-Saji, the hadith scholar of Basra, who supervised Ash'ari after his father's death. Under al-Saji, Ash'ari received a Sunni upbringing and learned the Salaf's position on God's attributes. The editor cites al-Dhahabi's praise of al-Saji and notes that Ash'ari relied on al-Saji's teaching in his works. The second teacher is Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab al-Jubba'i, the father (distinguished from his son), who was the leading Mu'tazili of Basra. Ash'ari's mother married al-Jubba'i after the death of Ash'ari's father, moving Ash'ari into a Mu'tazili environment. From about age ten, Ash'ari absorbed Mu'tazili thought and became a prominent defender of that school, even representing al-Jubba'i in debates. This biography explains Ash'ari's early theological formation.

Correction: In fact, Ash'ari was first a Sunni under al-Saji, then a Mu'tazili under al-Jubba'i, before his famous turn.

?

Al-Ash'ari's first teacher grounded him in Sunni tradition before he ever studied with the Mu'tazila.

This passage introduces the first teacher of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, Zakariyya al-Saji, a prominent hadith scholar of Basra. It reveals that al-Ash'ari was raised in a Sunni household and that his father entrusted his education to al-Saji. Understanding this early influence is crucial to seeing the trajectory of al-Ash'ari's thought.

Al-Ash'ari's Mu'tazili Period Under al-Jubba'i
Biographical note on al-Ash'ari's Mu'tazili period.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari's early environment shifted from Sunni to Mu'tazili under the influence of his stepfather, al-Jubba'i.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari was not always a Sunni; his Mu'tazili phase was shaped by his stepfather.

This passage opens a biographical window into al-Ash'ari's life before his famous turn to Sunni theology. It describes his early upbringing in a conservative Sunni household, which changed dramatically when his mother married the leading Mu'tazili scholar of Basra. Understanding this background is essential to appreciating why al-Ash'ari later became such a formidable defender of Mu'tazilism and, eventually, its most powerful critic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the narrative flow: the key events and figures.
The passage is a single continuous biographical note.
2
Identify biographical transitions
Notice the sequence: al-Ash'ari's early Sunni upbringing → mother's marriage to al-Jubba'i → move to Mu'tazili environment → adoption of Mu'tazilism → his role as deputy in debates.
These transitions set the stage for his later theological development.
3
Reflect on significance
Consider how this Mu'tazili period later influenced al-Ash'ari's own theological synthesis. Why do you think he eventually left Mu'tazilism?
This lesson only covers the Mu'tazili period; the reasons for his change come later.

As a result, he adopted Mu'tazilism and became a prominent defender of it, even serving as al-Jubba'i's deputy in major debates. This shows the significant influence of al-Jubba'i in shifting al-Ash'ari's theological orientation for a period.

Al-Ash'ari's Sunni Foundations and Diverse Teachers
Describes al-Ash'ari's retention of Sunni roots and his study with diverse Sunni teachers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Ash'ari's early Sunni upbringing was not erased by his Mu'tazili training, and that he studied with a wide range of Sunni scholars from various disciplines.

Core Idea
True scholarship builds on roots, not reactions.

This passage introduces al-Ash'ari's intellectual journey. Although his first teacher in kalam was the Mu'tazili al-Jubba'i, his earlier Sunni inheritance remained strong. After his maturity, he actively sought out Sunni shaykhs from hadith, Sufism, fiqh, and kalam, showing that his return to Sunnism was a synthesis, not a rejection.

Guided Path
1
Notice the continuity
The passage begins by stating that al-Jubba'i was his first teacher, but immediately qualifies that this did not erase his earlier Sunni roots. This shows that his later position was a development, not a sudden conversion.
2
Examine the diversity of teachers
List the categories of Sunni shaykhs mentioned: muhaddithun, sufis, fuqaha, mutakallimun. Note specific names like Ibn Kullab, al-Qalanisi, al-Muhasibi, al-Junayd. This demonstrates the breadth of his study.
3
Consider the implication
Why does the author emphasize this? Because al-Ash'ari's later work synthesizes these diverse streams. His methodology was not narrow but comprehensive.

ولا مراء في أن الجبائي هو أستاذه الأول في علم الكلام، ولكن ذلك لم يمْحُ مواريثه السنية الأولى، التي زكَّاها اتصاله المباشر وغير المباشر بعد نضجه - بشيوخ السنة : من محدثين وصوفية وفقهاء ومتكلمين؛ كابن كلاب، والقلانسي، والمحاسبي، وأبي القاسم الجنيد، وفقهاء الحنفية والشافعية والمالكية، حتى عده البعض مالكيًّا، وهو شافعي كما أحد ابن عساكر في «التبيين»، والسبكي في الطبقات (۱) - ولم يكن الرجل بعيدا عن دوائر الحنابلة وأهل الحديث، وقد نسب نفسه عند عودته إلى أهل السنة - إلى الإمام أحمد باعتباره زعيم أهل السنة، في كتاب «الإبانة» وغيره، كما ألم بمقالات الإسلاميين جميعا، من الفرق الإسلامية، والديانات والملل الأخرى، والمقالات الفلسفية المشائية وغيرها وعرض ذلك كله بأمانة وموضوعية في كتابه مقالات الإسلاميين، لكنه استوعب هذه الروافد المتنوعة، التي تبدو أنها لم تتيسر لمعاصِرَيْه وشريكيه في نصرة عقيدة أهل السنة والجماعة، بهذا التنوع والشمول وهما أبو منصور الماتريدي (۳۳۳هـ) فيما وراء النهر، وأبو جعفر الطحاوي (۳۲۱هـ) بمصر، كما ذكرنا آنفا، وفي السياق نفسه - وإن تأخرت نصف قرن تأتي جهود ابن أبي زيد القيرواني، في الجناح الغربي من العالم ، ومن قبله الإمامُ سُحنون -رضي الله عن الجميع .
And there is no doubt that al-Jubba'i was his first teacher in Kalam, but that did not erase his first Sunni inheritances, which were nurtured by his direct and indirect contact after his maturity - with the Sunni shaykhs: from hadith scholars, Sufis, jurists, and mutakallimun; such as Ibn Kullab, al-Qalanisi, al-Muhasibi, Abu al-Qasim al-Junayd, and the jurists of the Hanafis, Shafi'is, and Malikis, to the point that some considered him a Maliki, while he was a Shafi'i as stated by Ibn 'Asakir in 'al-Tabyin' and al-Subki in 'al-Tabaqat' (1). And the man was not far from the circles of the Hanabila and the people of hadith. And upon his return to the People of the Sunnah, he attributed himself to Imam Ahmad as the leader of the Sunnis, in the book 'al-Ibana' and others. He also comprehensively covered the articles of all Islamists, from Islamic sects, other religions and creeds, and Peripatetic philosophical articles, and presented all that with honesty and objectivity in his book 'Maqalat al-Islamiyyin'. But he assimilated these diverse sources, which seemed not to have been available to his contemporaries and partners in supporting the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, with this diversity and comprehensiveness, namely Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (d. 333 AH) beyond the river, and Abu Ja'far al-Tahawi (d. 321 AH) in Egypt, as we mentioned earlier. And in the same context - albeit half a century later - come the efforts of Ibn Abi Zayd al-Qayrawani in the western wing of the Arab world, and before him Imam Sahnun - may Allah be pleased with them all.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and methodological overview of al-Ash'ari's intellectual development and scholarly output. It first acknowledges that al-Jubba'i was al-Ash'ari's first teacher in Kalam, but emphasizes that this Mu'tazili background did not eradicate his earlier Sunni foundations. Instead, after reaching maturity, al-Ash'ari sought out and connected with Sunni shaykhs from various disciplines: hadith scholars, Sufis, jurists, and theologians like Ibn Kullab, al-Qalanisi, al-Muhasibi, and Abu al-Qasim al-Junayd. He also studied with jurists from all four madhhabs. The passage notes that some even thought he was Maliki, but he was actually Shafi'i. It also mentions his connection to Hanbali circles. Upon his return to Sunni Islam, he explicitly aligned himself with Imam Ahmad as the champion of Sunnism, as seen in his book 'al-Ibana'. Furthermore, the passage highlights his comprehensive knowledge of all Islamic sects and other religions and philosophies, which he presented objectively in 'Maqalat al-Islamiyyin'. The text concludes by comparing al-Ash'ari's comprehensive synthesis to that of his contemporaries al-Maturidi and al-Tahawi, as well as later figures like Ibn Abi Zayd al-Qayrawani and Sahnun, noting that al-Ash'ari's diverse sources were uniquely extensive.

Key Terms
شيوخ السنة
ابن كلاب
القلانسي
المحاسبي
الجنيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari completely abandoned his Mu'tazili training and started fresh.
Correction: The passage shows that he retained his early Sunni roots and built upon them, integrating diverse streams.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was al-Ash'ari's madhhab?
A) Maliki
B) Hanafi
C) Shafi'i
D) Hanbali

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's Sunni identity was rooted in his upbringing and reinforced by study with diverse Sunni masters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that al-Ash'ari's foundation in truth was laid before his exposure to deviance, and that foundation remained intact. What is the 'Sunni roots' in your own heart—those core beliefs and practices you received from family or early teachers—that you must guard, even as you encounter differing ideas? Write down one practical step you will take this week to reinforce that foundation.
?
Al-Ash'ari studied with critics of his own teacher and still came out stronger. Think of someone whose views challenge your faith: a friend, a professor, or an online voice. Have you allowed their influence to erode what you know is true, or have you, like al-Ash'ari, used that encounter to deepen your own roots? Be honest with yourself and commit to one specific way you will prevent that erosion starting today.
?
The passage names a wide range of Sunni teachers from different schools (Hanafis, Shafi'is, Malikis). Are you willing to learn from others within the broader Ahl al-Sunnah, even if they follow a different madhhab or spiritual path? Or do you suspect that seeking knowledge outside your own circle weakens your identity? If you find a prejudice in your heart, repent from it and resolve to seek knowledge wherever it is found, as long as it is rooted in the truth.

Al-Ash'ari's Alignment with Ahmad ibn Hanbal and Comprehensive Scholarship
Describes al-Ash'ari's connection to Hanbalis and his comprehensive work Maqalat al-Islamiyyin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Ash'ari aligned himself with Imam Ahmad as the leader of the Sunnis and that he produced a comprehensive survey of Islamic sects and other religions.

Core Idea
Scholarship requires both loyalty and breadth.

This passage continues the account of al-Ash'ari's return to Sunnism. It emphasizes his closeness to Hanbali circles and his explicit ascription to Imam Ahmad. Then it highlights his monumental work 'Maqalat al-Islamiyyin', where he objectively presented the doctrines of all Islamic sects, other religions, and even Greek philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify his alignment
Notice that he was not far from the circles of the Hanabila and the people of hadith. He attributed himself to Imam Ahmad as the leader of the Sunnis in his book al-Ibana.
This shows his explicit affiliation with Ahmad ibn Hanbal's legacy.
2
Examine the scope of his work
Read the list: he covered all Islamic sects, other religions and creeds, and Peripatetic philosophical articles. This demonstrates his comprehensive approach.
The word 'جميعا' emphasizes totality.
3
Appreciate the methodology
He presented all this with honesty and objectivity (بأمانة وموضوعية). This is a hallmark of his scholarly character.

?

Al-Ash'ari's Unique Synthesis Compared to Contemporaries
Compares al-Ash'ari's comprehensive sources to those of al-Maturidi and al-Tahawi, and mentions later Maghrebi scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Ash'ari had access to more diverse sources than his contemporaries al-Maturidi and al-Tahawi, and that later scholars like Ibn Abi Zayd al-Qayrawani and Sahnun continued his legacy.

Core Idea
Comprehensiveness is a gift and a responsibility.

The passage concludes by highlighting al-Ash'ari's unique advantage: he assimilated diverse intellectual sources that were not available to his fellow Sunni theologians al-Maturidi and al-Tahawi. This placed him in a distinct position. The author also notes that later scholars in other regions, like Ibn Abi Zayd and Sahnun, followed in his footsteps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the comparison
The passage compares al-Ash'ari with Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (in Transoxiana) and Abu Ja'far al-Tahawi (in Egypt). It says the diverse sources were not easily available to them.
This underscores al-Ash'ari's unique position in Iraq.
2
Understand the implication
Despite different contexts, all three were 'partners in supporting the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a'. So they shared the same goal but with different resources.
3
Note the later figures
Mention of Ibn Abi Zayd al-Qayrawani in the Maghreb and Imam Sahnun shows the spread of this comprehensive approach.
The author prays for them all, showing respect.

Al-Ash'ari's Sunni Teachers and His Intellectual Formation
Lists al-Ash'ari's Sunni teachers and corrects his school affiliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diverse Sunni influences that shaped al-Ash'ari's theology after his initial Mu'tazilite training.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's teachers ranged from theologians to Sufis, jurists to hadith scholars.

This passage reveals the rich network of Sunni masters who influenced al-Ash'ari after his early studies under al-Jubba'i. While al-Jubba'i was his first teacher in kalam, al-Ash'ari's later formation drew from a wide spectrum of Sunni traditions, including hadith scholars, Sufis, and jurists from multiple schools. This diversity prepared him for his eventual synthesis of Sunni creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on teachers
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the list of teachers and their affiliations.
Notice the variety: Ibn Kullab (theologian), al-Qalanisi (theologian), al-Muhasibi (Sufi), al-Junayd (Sufi), and jurists.
2
Identify the correction of a misconception
Find where the passage corrects the mistaken belief that al-Ash'ari was Maliki. Note the sources cited.
Ibn 'Asakir and al-Subki confirm he was Shafi'i.
3
Reflect on the significance of diversity
Consider why having teachers from different Sunni schools mattered for al-Ash'ari's later work.
This breadth allowed him to address a wide range of theological issues.

والمواجهات الكبرى، ولا مراء في أن الجبائي هو أستاذه الأول في علم الكلام، ولكن ذلك لم يمْحُ مواريثه السنية الأولى، التي زكَّاها اتصاله المباشر وغير المباشر بعد نضجه - بشيوخ السنة : من محدثين وصوفية وفقهاء ومتكلمين؛ كابن كلاب، والقلانسي، والمحاسبي، وأبي القاسم الجنيد، وفقهاء الحنفية والشافعية والمالكية، حتى عده البعض مالكيًّا، وهو شافعي كما حكى ابن عساكر في «التبيين»، والسبكي في الطبقات - ولم يكن الرجل بعيدا عن دوائر الحنابلة وأهل الحديث، وقد نسب نفسه عند عودته إلى أهل السنة - إلى الإمام أحمد باعتباره زعيم أهل السنة، في كتاب «الإبانة» وغيره، كما ألم بمقالات الإسلاميين جميعا، من الفرق الإسلامية، والديانات والملل الأخرى، والمقالات الفلسفية المشائية وغيرها وعرض ذلك كله بأمانة وموضوعية في كتابه مقالات الإسلاميين، لكنه استوعب هذه الروافد المتنوعة، التي تبدو أنها لم تتيسر لمعاصِرَيْه وشريكيه في نصرة عقيدة أهل السنة والجماعة، بهذا التنوع والشمول وهما أبو منصور الماتريدي (٣٣٣هـ) فيما وراء النهر، وأبو جعفر الطحاوي (٣٢١هـ) بمصر، كما ذكرنا آنفا، وفي السياق نفسه - وإن تأخرت نصف قرن تأتي جهود ابن أبي زيد القيرواني، في الجناح الغربي من العالم ، ومن قبله الإمامُ سُحنون -رضي الله عن الجميع .
And the great confrontations, and there is no doubt that al-Jubba'i is his first teacher in theology, but that did not erase his first Sunni inheritances, which were purified by his direct and indirect connection after his maturity - with the Sunni masters: from hadith scholars, Sufis, jurists, and theologians; such as Ibn Kullab, al-Qalanisi, al-Muhasibi, Abu al-Qasim al-Junayd, and the jurists of the Hanafis, Shafi'is, and Malikis, so that some counted him as Maliki, while he was Shafi'i as Ibn 'Asakir narrated in 'al-Tabyin' and al-Subki in 'al-Tabaqat' - and he was not far from the circles of the Hanbalis and the People of Hadith, and he attributed himself upon his return to Ahl al-Sunna - to Imam Ahmad as the leader of Ahl al-Sunna, in the book 'al-Ibana' and others. He also encompassed all the doctrines of the Muslims, from the Islamic sects, other religions and creeds, and the Peripatetic philosophical doctrines and others, and presented all that with honesty and objectivity in his book 'Maqalat al-Islamiyyin'. But he absorbed these diverse tributaries, which seem not to have been available to his two contemporaries and partners in supporting the creed of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, with this diversity and comprehensiveness, namely Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (333 AH) in Transoxiana, and Abu Ja'far al-Tahawi (321 AH) in Egypt, as we mentioned earlier. And in the same context - though delayed half a century - come the efforts of Ibn Abi Zayd al-Qayrawani in the western wing of the world, and before him Imam Sahnun - may God be pleased with them all.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and comparative analysis of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's intellectual formation. It begins by acknowledging that al-Jubba'i was his first teacher in theology, but emphasizes that this did not erase his earlier Sunni foundations. These foundations were cultivated through his direct and indirect contact with various Sunni masters, including hadith scholars, Sufis, jurists, and theologians: Ibn Kullab, al-Qalanisi, al-Muhasibi, Abu al-Qasim al-Junayd, and jurists from the Hanafi, Shafi'i, and Maliki schools. Some people mistakenly considered him Maliki, but the author corrects this by citing Ibn 'Asakir and al-Subki that he was Shafi'i. The passage also notes that al-Ash'ari was not distant from Hanbali and Hadith circles, and upon his return to Sunni orthodoxy, he affiliated himself with Imam Ahmad, regarding him as the leader of Ahl al-Sunna, as seen in his book 'al-Ibana'. Furthermore, the text highlights al-Ash'ari's comprehensive knowledge of all Islamic sects, other religions, and philosophical doctrines, which he presented impartially in his 'Maqalat al-Islamiyyin'. This unique breadth, the passage argues, was not available to his contemporaries who also championed Sunni orthodoxy: Abu Mansur al-Maturidi in the east and Abu Ja'far al-Tahawi in Egypt. The passage concludes by mentioning later figures in the same tradition: Ibn Abi Zayd al-Qayrawani and Imam Sahnun.

المحاسبي
الجنيد
ابن عساكر
السبكي
الإبانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari was Maliki in jurisprudence.
Correction: He was Shafi'i, as confirmed by Ibn 'Asakir and al-Subki.

Checkpoint
Who was al-Ash'ari's first teacher in theology according to the passage?
A) Ibn Kullab
B) Al-Jubba'i
C) Al-Muhasibi
D) Al-Junayd

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's theology was shaped by a diverse network of Sunni teachers, correcting the notion that he remained solely Mu'tazilite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ash'ari sought teachers from hadith, Sufism, fiqh, and kalam. Which of these four areas is weakest in your own religious education? What specific step will you take this week to learn from someone who excels in that area?
?
The passage corrects a common mistake about al-Ash'ari's madhhab with clear evidence. Is there a misconception about your own beliefs or practices that you have accepted without verification? What source will you consult today to confirm or correct it?
?
Al-Ash'ari's first teacher was a Mu'tazilite, yet he did not limit himself to that school. Are you allowing one influential teacher or book to exclusively shape your understanding of Islam, to the exclusion of other Sunni traditions? How will you broaden your sources starting now?

Al-Ash'ari's Comprehensive Work: Maqalat al-Islamiyyin
Describes the content and method of al-Ash'ari's book Maqalat al-Islamiyyin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the scope and impartiality of al-Ash'ari's Maqalat al-Islamiyyin as a survey of diverse beliefs.

Core Idea
Maqalat al-Islamiyyin objectively documented all Islamic sects and beyond.

The passage highlights al-Ash'ari's encyclopedic work 'Maqalat al-Islamiyyin' as a unique achievement. He not only covered Islamic sects but also other religions and philosophical traditions. This lesson focuses on the character of that work and its value for understanding the theological landscape of his time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Maqalat
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the description of the book's content.
Note the three categories: Islamic sects, other religions, and philosophical doctrines.
2
Analyze the method
Identify how al-Ash'ari presented these views – with honesty and objectivity.
The passage uses 'بأمانة وموضوعية' (honestly and objectively).
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why such a comprehensive and impartial survey was important for defending Sunni orthodoxy.
Knowing opponents' views allows more effective refutation.

Al-Ash'ari's Unique Breadth Compared to Maturidi and Tahawi
Compares al-Ash'ari's comprehensive sources with Maturidi and Tahawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari's access to diverse sources set him apart from his contemporaries al-Maturidi and al-Tahawi.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's unique access to varied Sunni and non-Sunni sources distinguished him from Maturidi and Tahawi.

The passage concludes by comparing al-Ash'ari with two other major Sunni theologians: Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and Abu Ja'far al-Tahawi. While all three defended Sunni orthodoxy, al-Ash'ari had access to a wider range of sources and schools. This lesson explores that comparison and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the comparison passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the comparison and the mention of later figures.
Note that Maturidi and Tahawi are named as contemporaries.
2
Identify the difference
What did al-Ash'ari have that they lacked? The passage says 'these diverse tributaries'.
These tributaries refer to the varied Sunni teachers and exposure to other religions and philosophies.
3
Consider the geographical context
Think about why Maturidi in Transoxiana and Tahawi in Egypt might not have had the same exposure.
Al-Ash'ari was in Baghdad, a center of intellectual exchange.
4
Note later figures
The passage mentions Ibn Abi Zayd and Sahnun as later continuators of the same tradition.

Al-Jubba'i's Influence on al-Ash'ari
Analyzes al-Jubba'i's role in al-Ash'ari's development.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jubba'i's tutelage shaped al-Ash'ari's intellectual formation, despite al-Ash'ari's later opposition and refutation of his teacher's works.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's greatest teacher also became his greatest intellectual sparring partner.

This passage highlights the profound yet complex relationship between al-Ash'ari and his Mu'tazili teacher al-Jubba'i. It also introduces al-Jubba'i's son, Abu Hashim, and the Bahshamiyya sect. Understanding this relationship is key to appreciating al-Ash'ari's intellectual maturity and his eventual break from Mu'tazilism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Jubba'i
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the three figures mentioned: al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim, and al-Ash'ari himself.
Notice that al-Jubba'i is described as formative despite later opposition.
2
Understand the nuance of influence
The passage emphasizes that al-Jubba'i's role cannot be belittled even after al-Ash'ari wrote refutations. Why would a refutation still indicate influence?
Think about how knowing a system well allows you to critique it effectively.
3
Reflect on the deputization
Al-Jubba'i would deputize al-Ash'ari in delicate debates. What does this say about their trust and al-Ash'ari's skill?
This trust likely sharpened al-Ash'ari's own arguments.
4
Connect to al-Ash'ari's maturity
The passage concludes that this relationship matured al-Ash'ari's personality. How might intellectual opposition to a respected teacher contribute to personal growth?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يمكن التهوين من دور الجبائي في التكوين الفكري لأبي الحسن، برغم ما ألَّفه بعد تركه الاعتزال، نقضا على مؤلفات شيخه السابق، وكذا الجبائي الأصغر أبو هاشم (۳۲۱هـ) زميله، وصاحبه في التلمذة لهذا الشيخ، وهو الجبائي الابن الذي بقي على الاعتزال طول عمره، والذي انفرد بفرقة «البهشمية» فيما بعد. وقد تطرق مؤرخو الإمام، وخصوصًا الذهبي والسبكي على ما بينهما من خلاف إلى شخصية الجبائي العلمية، وتأثيره في أبي الحسن، وما كان بينهما أول الأمر من ثقة متبادلة، حتى كان ينيب تلميذه، الذي برع في الجدل، عنه في المواقف الدقيقة . ولا شك أن هذا رغم اختلاف الوجهة فيما بعد قد أنضج شخصية أبي الحسن. أما الشيخ زكريا الساجي فقد كان تأثيره أيام النشأة، ولذا ظل مختزنا في عقل الشيخ، حتى انتفض أخيرا في الفترة «البرزخية»، التي تحول فيها الرجل عن الاعتزال إلى الفكر السُّنّي، وهي ليست بالتأكيد مجرد أسبوعين، وإنما هو صراع امتد -حسب طبائع الأمور فترة ليست بالقصيرة حتى انتهى فيها إلى إعلان موقفه، وعودته إلى رحاب أهل السنة والجماعة، منتصف العقد الأخير من القرن الثالث الهجري، بالبصرة.
And the role of al-Jubba'i in the intellectual formation of Abu al-Hasan cannot be belittled, despite what he authored after leaving Mu'tazilism, as refutations of his former teacher's works, and also al-Jubba'i the younger Abu Hashim (321 AH), his companion and fellow student under this teacher, and he is the son of al-Jubba'i who remained a Mu'tazilite his entire life, and who later uniquely founded the 'Bahshamiyya' sect. The historians of the Imam, especially al-Dhahabi and al-Subki, despite their differences, have discussed the scholarly character of al-Jubba'i, his influence on Abu al-Hasan, and the mutual trust that existed between them initially, to the point that he would deputize his student, who excelled in debate, for him in delicate situations. There is no doubt that this, despite the later divergence, matured Abu al-Hasan's personality. As for Shaykh Zakariyya al-Saji, his influence was during his upbringing, and so it remained stored in the shaykh's mind, until it finally erupted during the 'intermediate' (barzakhiyya) period, in which the man turned from Mu'tazilism to Sunni thought. It was certainly not just two weeks, but rather a struggle that extended - according to the nature of things - a not-short period, until he ended up declaring his position and returning to the fold of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, in the middle of the last decade of the third century AH, in Basra.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage continues the biographical analysis by focusing on al-Ash'ari's relationship with his Mu'tazili teacher al-Jubba'i and the process of his conversion. It begins by stating that al-Jubba'i's role in al-Ash'ari's intellectual formation cannot be underestimated, even though al-Ash'ari later wrote refutations of his teacher's works. Also mentioned is al-Jubba'i al-Asghar (Abu Hashim), his fellow student and the son of al-Jubba'i, who remained a Mu'tazilite and founded the Bahshamiyya sect. Historians like al-Dhahabi and al-Subki note the mutual trust between al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i, such that al-Jubba'i would deputize his skilled student in delicate debates. Despite their later divergence, this relationship matured al-Ash'ari's personality. The passage then highlights another influence: Zakariyya al-Saji, whose impact during his early years remained dormant until the 'barzakhiyya' (intermediate) period, when al-Ash'ari underwent a transformation from Mu'tazilism to Sunni thought. This transformation was not a sudden event but a prolonged struggle, culminating in his public declaration and return to Ahl al-Sunna in Basra around the mid-290s AH.

Key Terms
الجبائي
أبو هاشم
البهشمية
الذهبي
السبكي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did al-Jubba'i deputize al-Ash'ari in delicate situations?
A) Because al-Ash'ari was his son
B) Because al-Ash'ari excelled in debate
C) Because al-Jubba'i was too busy
D) Because al-Ash'ari had already converted

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's intellectual influence on al-Ash'ari was foundational, and their relationship, though later adversarial, contributed to al-Ash'ari's intellectual maturity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that al-Ash'ari's intellectual maturity came partly through his relationship with a teacher he later disagreed with. Who in your life has shaped your thinking, even if you now hold different views? How are you allowing that tension to refine—not weaken—your convictions?
?
Al-Jubba'i entrusted al-Ash'ari with delicate debates, implying trust despite future conflict. Is there someone you currently disagree with whom you can still respect and learn from? What would it cost you to acknowledge their influence on you?
?
The historians note the mutual trust that allowed al-Ash'ari to represent his teacher. Do you have the courage to let those who might one day critique your ideas learn from you now? Write down one concrete step you can take this week to mentor someone without fear of their future independence.

The Journey from Mu'tazilism to Sunni Islam
Describes al-Ash'ari's conversion process and its duration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Ash'ari's conversion to Sunni theology was a gradual and prolonged struggle, not a sudden decision.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's return to Sunni Islam was a long, internal battle, not a two-week change.

This lesson examines the second part of the biographical passage, which focuses on al-Ash'ari's conversion process. It highlights the role of his early teacher Zakariyya al-Saji and the 'barzakhiyya' (intermediate) period of spiritual and intellectual struggle. The passage corrects the common misconception that al-Ash'ari's conversion happened in just two weeks, emphasizing instead a lengthy process culminating in his public declaration in Basra around the mid-290s AH.

Guided Path
1
Read about Zakariyya al-Saji's influence
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note how Zakariyya al-Saji's influence from al-Ash'ari's upbringing remained dormant until it erupted during the barzakhiyya period.
This suggests seeds of Sunni thought were planted early.
2
Understand the barzakhiyya period
The term 'barzakhiyya' literally means 'intermediate' or 'isthmus.' Here it describes a transitional period of struggle. The passage stresses it was not just two weeks but a lengthy conflict.
This corrects a popular misunderstanding.
3
Note the timeline and location
The passage dates the conversion to the mid-last decade of the third century AH in Basra. This provides a concrete historical anchor.

Al-Ash'ari's Madhhab: Shafi'i, Not Maliki
Al-Marwazi as teacher and clarification of al-Ash'ari's madhhab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Ash'ari studied Shafi'i fiqh under al-Marwazi and that the claim he was Maliki is a confusion with al-Baqillani.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari learned Shafi'i fiqh from al-Marwazi; the Maliki claim is a confusion with al-Baqillani.

This passage clarifies al-Ash'ari's fiqh affiliation by naming his teacher al-Marwazi, a Shafi'i jurist, and addresses a historical confusion where some thought al-Ash'ari was Maliki. The author traces that error to a mix-up with the later Ash'ari theologian al-Baqillani, who was indeed Maliki.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the two key pieces of information: the teacher and the clarification.
The passage is short but contains a clear correction.
2
Identify al-Marwazi's role
The passage states that al-Marwazi was a jurist, usuli, and theologian, and that al-Ash'ari learned Shafi'i fiqh from him. This directly shows al-Ash'ari's fiqh madhhab.
Al-Marwazi is not a well-known figure, but his role here is pivotal.
3
Understand the confusion
Some Maghrebis claimed al-Ash'ari was Maliki. The author says this is a confusion with al-Baqillani, who was a Maliki. This highlights the importance of distinguishing between early Ash'ari figures.
Al-Baqillani is a major Ash'ari theologian, but he followed the Maliki school.

Source Text

ثم المَرْوزي، الفقيه الأصولي المتكلم : ويعد في شيوخه أيضًا : «المروزي» أبو إسحاق إبراهيم بن أحمد (٣٤٠ هـ)، وقد أخذ منه الشيخ الفقه الشافعي، وقد ذكر بعض المغاربة أن الأشعري كان مالكيًّا، ويبدو أنه التباس مع الباقلاني.

And some Maghrebis mentioned that al-Ash'ari was Maliki, and it appears that it is a confusion with al-Baqillani.

Commentary
This passage adds another teacher to al-Ash'ari's list: al-Marwazi (Abu Ishaq Ibrahim ibn Ahmad, d. 340 AH), a jurist, usuli (legal theorist), and theologian. The passage states that al-Ash'ari studied Shafi'i fiqh under him, reinforcing that his madhhab was Shafi'i. It then addresses a common confusion: some people from the Maghreb claim that al-Ash'ari was Maliki. The author suggests this is a mix-up with al-Baqillani, a later Ash'ari theologian who was indeed Maliki. Thus, the passage serves to correct a historical misconception.
